    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="e888887b544347cda9506cd3323c1a28">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '1d37c074-f46e-45dd-bc27-dd5819f5cab8'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Lv/Hongyu">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Ma/Xiaojian" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a5" data-db-target-for="a5" aria-controls="a5" aria-haspopup="true" id="a5_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Xiaojian Ma</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a5" aria-labelledby="a5_Ctrl" role="region" id="a5">
                     <p class="author-name">Xiaojian Ma</p>
                     <p>Key Laboratory of Colloid and Interface Chemistry, Ministry of Education, School of Chemistry and Chemical Engineering, Shandong University, Jinan, 250100 P.R. China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Ma/Xiaojian">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Yang/Jian" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a6" data-db-target-for="a6" aria-controls="a6" aria-haspopup="true" id="a6_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Prof. Jian Yang<i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-mail_outline"></i></span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a6" aria-labelledby="a6_Ctrl" role="region" id="a6">
                     <p class="author-type mb-1">Corresponding Author</p>
                     <p class="author-name">Prof. Jian Yang</p>
                     <ul class="rlist sm-account">
                        <li class="sm-account__item"><a class="sm-account__link" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:yangjian@sdu.edu.cn"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-ic_mail_solid"></i><span>yangjian@sdu.edu.cn</span></a></li>
                     </ul>
                     <p>Key Laboratory of Colloid and Interface Chemistry, Ministry of Education, School of Chemistry and Chemical Engineering, Shandong University, Jinan, 250100 P.R. China</p>

         </div>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en">
         <h2 id="d29173913" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>A protocol is proposed for screening potential electrolyte additives from a number of siloxane molecules for high-temperature sodium-ion batteries. Octamethyltrisiloxane (MDM), as a result of this screening, greatly improves the high-temperature performance due to its preferential binding with anions, high reactivity with H<sub>2</sub>O/HF, and good interface stabilization.

            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5ba2cb0f-5816-4960-b432-e3931364c53e/anie202420573-gra-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/5ba2cb0f-5816-4960-b432-e3931364c53e/anie202420573-gra-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/512265d8-4299-4840-961d-d25af2ada73b/anie202420573-gra-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5ba2cb0f-5816-4960-b432-e3931364c53e/anie202420573-gra-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure><p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d29173995" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Sodium-ion batteries hold great promise for large-scale energy storage but face significant challenges at extreme temperatures. Especially at high temperatures, side reactions and electrode dissolution are exacerbated by repeated cycling. It is, therefore, essential to optimize the electrolyte formulation. Here, a series of siloxane molecules are screened to boost the high-temperature performance of the anode materials in SIBs. Octamethyltrisiloxane (MDM), as a result of this screening, prefers to interact with anions due to the unique electron-donating of Si→CH<sub>3</sub>. This association then weakens the P─F bond and facilitates the formation of a NaF-rich solid electrolyte interphase. Additionally, MDM inhibits the hydrolysis of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>, reducing the deleterious species in the electrolyte. Therefore, commercial Sn microparticles show a reversible capacity of 752 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 60&nbsp;°C after 750 cycles at 2 A g<sup>−1</sup>, much better than the case without MDM. This result exceeds the reported data at high temperatures. MDM as an electrolyte additive also improves the high-temperature performance of Bi and hard carbon. The results provide useful guidance for the rational selection of electrolyte additives for high-temperature sodium-ion batteries.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202420573-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202420573-sec-0010-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The rapid development of renewable energy sources, such as solar, wind, and tidal energy, greatly stimulates the advances of electrochemical energy storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202420573-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0001_R_d29173719e337" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Energy Environ. Sci.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), as one of the next-generation batteries, have become prevalent in recent years, due to their abundant resources in nature and high similarity to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202420573-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0002_R_d29173719e342" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Energy Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, the widespread applications of SIBs have to face harsh environments in the real world, for example, deserts, polar regions, and deep sea/space as well. For instance, at high temperatures, the side reactions are significantly exacerbated, which deteriorates the interface stability between electrolyte and electrode and results in the repeated formation/destruction of solid electrolyte interphase (SEI).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202420573-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0003_R_d29173719e347" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - ACS Energy Lett.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Then, the limited electrolyte is continuously consumed, and the electrochemical performance gradually degrades. This degradation is particularly severe for SIBs due to the high reactivity of sodiated electrodes and the high solubility of Na salts.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202420573-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0004_R_d29173719e353" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nat. Energy">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the exploration about the high-temperature performance of SIBs is much less than that at room temperature.</p>
         
         <p>Because these issues are closely related to electrolytes, the solution is to design and select appropriate electrolytes for high-temperature SIBs. To date, there are two popular electrolytes for SIBs, ester-based and ether-based electrolytes. Compared to the former case, ether-based ones show remarkable advantages because they can form a thin and robust SEI on the surface of anode materials to maintain good interfacial stability and improve the charge-transfer kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202420573-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0005_R_d29173719e361" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, ether-based electrolytes are seldom reported for high-temperature SIBs. The limited reports mainly focus on hard carbon (HC).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202420573-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0006_R_d29173719e366" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - ACS Energy Lett.">6</a>-<a href="#anie202420573-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0008_R_d29173719e366" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Compared with HC, alloy-type anode materials have higher theoretical capacities, superior electron conductivities, and larger volume densities, which increase the energy density of SIBs. However, the reports using alloy-type anode materials for high-temperature SIBs are scarce<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202420573-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0009_R_d29173719e371" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">9</a>-<a href="#anie202420573-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0011_R_d29173719e371" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Energy Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> because the huge volume change makes stable cycling very difficult even at room temperature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202420573-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0012_R_d29173719e377" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nano Lett.">12</a>-<a href="#anie202420573-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0015_R_d29173719e377" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Recently, Li's group reported that solvated Na<sup>+</sup> was reversibly inserted or extracted from layer-structured Bi, thereby achieving a wide temperature range for SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202420573-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0009_R_d29173719e384" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The full cells of Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> @C||Bi delivered a capacity of ∼265&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (anode mass) after 100 cycles at 60&nbsp;°C. Yang and coworkers synthesized flower-like tin nanorods (Sn@NC) coated with nitrogen-doped carbon by in situ polymerization and high-temperature reduction in H<sub>2</sub>/Ar.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202420573-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0010_R_d29173719e407" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Energy Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The synergistic effect of N-doped carbon on Sn nanorods and ether-based electrolyte enabled the full cells of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C||Sn@NC to achieve a reversible capacity of ∼49 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (cathode mass) and a capacity retention of ∼70% after 500 cycles at 50&nbsp;°C. To date, the high-temperature performance is still far away to be satisfied.</p>
         
         <p>Using appropriate electrolyte additives also improves the high-temperature performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202420573-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0016_R_d29173719e426" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Chem. Rev.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among various candidates, siloxane analogues are attractive due to their ability to remove harmful species from the electrolyte at high temperatures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202420573-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0017_R_d29173719e431" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Energy Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For instance, Liang's group reported tris(trimethylsilyl) phosphite (Si─O─P, TMSPi) as an electrolyte additive to improve the electrochemical performance of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F||HC over a wide temperature range.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202420573-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0018_R_d29173719e447" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The underlying mechanism was attributed to the strong binding between TMSPi and Na<sup>+</sup>, which changes the solvation structures of Na<sup>+</sup> and cooperates with fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) to enhance the film-forming ability. Jiang et al. tried <i>N</i>, <i>O</i>-bis(trimethylsilyl) trifluoroacetamide (Si─O─C, BSTFA) as an electrolyte additive to elevate the electrochemical performance of sodium metal batteries (SMBs).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202420573-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0019_R_d29173719e461" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Energy Storage Mater.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> BSTFA effectively scavenged H<sub>2</sub>O and HF in the electrolyte and regulated the electrode interfaces, promoting the electrochemical performance of SMBs at high temperatures. These advances indicate the promising potential of siloxane molecules for high-temperature SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202420573-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0020_R_d29173719e469" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> So far, there are a number of siloxane molecules that have not been tested. Testing them one-by-one is time-consuming and labor intensive. It is necessary to achieve rational screening for the siloxane family. Moreover, the electrolyte chemistry associated with Si─O─Si, which is popular in the siloxane family, has not been investigated yet. It may be different from those of Si─O─P and Si─O─C. Finally, siloxane molecules are not applied to alloy-type anode materials of SIBs at high temperatures, to our knowledge.</p>
         
         <p>Here, a protocol is proposed to screen the potential electrolyte additives from a series of siloxane molecules for high-temperature SIBs. Octamethyltrisiloxane (MDM), as a result of this screening, is used to further dig into the underlying insights for the improved performance. 1) MDM prefers to interact with anions rather than Na<sup>+</sup> in the electrolyte. Therefore, it weakens the binding of P─F, promotes the dissociation of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>, and facilitates the formation of NaF-rich SEI. The role of anion acceptor is reported in siloxane for the first time. 2) MDM also efficiently cleans H<sub>2</sub>O and HF in the electrolyte, and reduces the electrolyte corrosion. Benefited from these effects, the SEI dissolution is inhibited in the presence of MDM, which received little attention in previous reports. The multiple roles of MDM, that is, acid/water removal, film formation, and anion acceptor, work together to improve the high-temperature performance of SIBs. This improvement is observed for different anode materials, demonstrating the good compatibility of MDM. These results provide useful guidance for molecule design and selection of electrolyte additives.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202420573-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202420573-sec-0020-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>A number of siloxane molecules are investigated to clarify their effect on the electrochemical performance of SIBs in a panorama. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202420573-fig-0001">1a</a> shows the molecular structures of siloxane candidates, such as methoxytrimethylsilane (MOTMS), dimethyldimethoxysilane (DMOS), methyltrimethoxysilane (MTMS), hexamethyldisiloxane (MM), octamethyltrisiloxane (MDM), decamethyltetrasiloxane (MD<sub>2</sub>M), hexamethylcyclotrisiloxane (D<sub>3</sub>), and octamethylcyclotetrasiloxane (D<sub>4</sub>). The siloxane molecules are rationally screened as an electrolyte additive for high-temperature applications. First, the electrochemical stability of these siloxane molecules, which is an important trait of electrolytes, is evaluated by the energy levels of the lowest unoccupied molecular orbits (LUMO) and the highest occupied molecular orbits (HOMO).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202420573-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0021_R_d29173719e502" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - ACS Nano">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202420573-fig-0001">1b</a>, MDM is located in the lower right corner of this figure, indicating its relatively low LUMO and relatively high HOMO energy level. This fact implies that MDM may decompose during cycles, participating in the formation of the SEI. It then prevents particle pulverization, solvent co-intercalation, potential SEI dissolution, and cathode corrosion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202420573-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0022_R_d29173719e511" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Energy Environ. Sci.">22</a>, <a href="#anie202420573-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0023_R_d29173719e511" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Second, the ability to clean hazardous species, for example, HF, H<sub>2</sub>O, and so on, from the electrolyte is examined by the binding energy.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202420573-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0024_R_d29173719e519" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Energy Storage Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This ability is especially important at high temperature because the hydrolysis/corrosion reactions induced by these species are remarkably exacerbated, and the electrode degradation is accelerated accordingly.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202420573-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0025_R_d29173719e524" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202420573-fig-0001">1c</a>, MDM is located in the lower left corner of this figure, indicating the strong binding with H<sub>2</sub>O and HF simultaneously. The results suggest that MDM is more likely to adsorb and react with these species, thereby suppressing the hydrolysis of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> and then the formation of HF in the electrolyte.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202420573-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0026_R_d29173719e538" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Energy Mater.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the corrosion of cathode materials and SEI components is mitigated. This conclusion is supported by the experimental data. Using MM, MDM, and MD<sub>2</sub>M as a comparison, the signal of HF in these electrolytes after 1 day at 60&nbsp;°C follows the order of the binding energy (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>). Third, the additive needs to have a high boiling point and a large ionic conductivity for high-temperature applications. As depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202420573-fig-0001">1d</a>, MDM comes out in front in two terms. Taking all of these results into account (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202420573-fig-0001">1b–d</a>), MDM becomes a promising electrolyte additive for the high-temperature performance.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202420573-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ba59c8aa-0285-4a12-b019-62426bfae5b8/anie202420573-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/ba59c8aa-0285-4a12-b019-62426bfae5b8/anie202420573-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c7ae1d5d-8e17-4215-9944-7a5b3525fd14/anie202420573-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ba59c8aa-0285-4a12-b019-62426bfae5b8/anie202420573-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202420573-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202420573" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Screening protocol of potential siloxane molecules for high-temperature SIBs. a) The molecule formula, b) the energy levels of HOMO and LUMO, c) the binding energies with H<sub>2</sub>O and HF, and d) the boiling points of siloxane molecules and the ionic conductivities as the electrolyte additives. e) Comparison of the cycling performances of μ-Sn using different siloxane molecules as the electrolyte additives. The same temperature and the same concentration of siloxane (1 wt%) were adopted for the comparison.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To verify the above conclusion, we examine different siloxane molecules as the electrolyte additives for high-temperature SIBs, using commercial microparticles of Sn (μ-Sn) as a model. The selection of Sn is due to its high specific capacity (847 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), excellent electronic conductivity (8.7&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>6</sup> S m<sup>−1</sup>), and low operating voltage (∼0.3&nbsp;V) as an anode material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202420573-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0012_R_d29173719e586" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nano Lett.">12</a>-<a href="#anie202420573-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0015_R_d29173719e586" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">15</a>, <a href="#anie202420573-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0027_R_d29173719e586" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J. Energy Chem.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, μ-Sn cycled in BE (basic electrolyte) + 1 wt% MDM delivers a reversible capacity of 784 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and a capacity retention of 99.1% after 300 cycles at 2.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> and at 60&nbsp;°C. This result is much better than that in BE. Meanwhile, this result also wins out in the comparison of other siloxane molecules (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202420573-fig-0001">1e</a> and Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3–S6</a>), confirming that the above screening protocol is effective. Although using DMOS or D<sub>3</sub> as an electrolyte additive also shows the good cycling stability for μ-Sn, the overall performance, such as specific capacity, initial Coulombic Efficiency (iCE), rate capability, and so on, is still inferior to that using MDM. Therefore, the following discussion will focus on MDM.</p>
         
         <p>In order to further clarify the underlying insights for this improved performance, a series of theoretical calculations and spectral characterizations were carried out using MDM as a model. First, the solvation structures and coordination spheres of Na<sup>+</sup>/PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> ions were evaluated by molecular dynamics (MD) simulations. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202420573-fig-0002">2a</a>, Na<sup>+</sup> ions are typically surrounded by two DGM molecules and one PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> as a solvation structure in BE+MDM at high temperatures. MDM does not appear in this solvation sheath, which is also supported by the radial distribution functions (RDFs) of Na<sup>+</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202420573-fig-0002">2b</a>). Compared to the case without MDM (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>), the coordination number (CN) of Na⋯F(PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>) slightly decreases from 2.24 of BE to 1.99 of BE+MDM, but the CN of Na⋯O(DGM) increases from 4.83 of BE to 5.06 of BE+MDM, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202420573-fig-0002">2c</a>. These changes indicate that some of the PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> in the solvation structure is replaced by DGM in BE+MDM. This fact can be understood by the weak binding energy of Na<sup>+</sup>⋯MDM (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202420573-fig-0002">2d</a>). The binding energy of Na<sup>+</sup>⋯MDM is only −5.02&nbsp;kcal mol<sup>−1</sup>, much lower than that of Na<sup>+</sup>⋯DGM (−17.6&nbsp;kcal mol<sup>−1</sup>) and Na<sup>+</sup>⋯PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> (−32.0&nbsp;kcal mol<sup>−1</sup>). This result, together with the low amount of MDM, makes it difficult for MDM to access the solvation sheath of Na<sup>+</sup>. To clarify the status of MDM in the electrolyte, its interactions with other species are calculated and summarized in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202420573-fig-0002">2e</a>. It is noticed that the binding energy of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>⋯MDM (−8.91&nbsp;kcal mol<sup>−1</sup>) is higher than that of DGM⋯MDM (−5.39&nbsp;kcal mol<sup>−1</sup>) and Na<sup>+</sup>⋯MDM (−5.02&nbsp;kcal mol<sup>−1</sup>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202420573-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0028_R_d29173719e681" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These results indicate that MDM prefers to bind PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> rather than Na<sup>+</sup> and DGM. This conclusion is indirectly supported by the binding energies of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>⋯MDM and PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>⋯DGM (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>). However, the limited quantity of MDM cannot saturate all the coordination sites around PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> so that there are still many DGM molecules near PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>, as revealed by the RDFs of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>). In short, MDM does not participate in the solvation sheath of Na<sup>+</sup>, but acts as an anion acceptor in the electrolyte. Independent gradient model analysis based on the Hirshfeld partition of molecular density (IGMH) shows that MDM attracts PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> mainly through hydrogen bonding and van der Waals forces (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202420573-fig-0002">2f,g</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202420573-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0029_R_d29173719e723" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Nano-Micro Lett.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The interaction mainly occurs between F of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> and ─CH<sub>3</sub> of MDM. Compared to the case of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>⋯MDM, the weak interaction of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>⋯DGM can be attributed to the reduced positive charge on ─OCH<sub>3</sub> induced by the electron withdrawal effect of oxygen (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202420573-fig-0002">2h</a>). This strong attraction of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>⋯MDM pulls PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> out and allows more DGM molecules to get into the solvation sheath. As a result, the bond lengths of P─F increase in BE+MDM (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202420573-fig-0002">2i</a>), which favors the anion decomposition and promotes the formation of inorganic-rich SEI.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202420573-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0030_R_d29173719e756" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Energy Storage Mater.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The changes in the solvation structures are supported by Raman spectra and <sup>19</sup>F-nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202420573-fig-0002">2j</a> shows the Raman spectra of BE+MDM, BE, and NaPF<sub>6</sub> in the range of 730–780&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> at 60&nbsp;°C, where the peak can be attributed to the vibration of P─F in PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202420573-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0031_R_d29173719e774" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - ACS Energy Lett.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> When NaPF<sub>6</sub> is dissolved in DGM, the peak of P─F shifts to the low wavenumbers compared to the case in the NaPF<sub>6</sub> salt, which is due to the formation of the solvation structures of Na<sup>+</sup> and the interaction between PF<sub>6</sub><sup>ˉ</sup> and DGM. This peak shifts further to the low wavenumber after the addition of MDM, due to the enhanced interaction between PF<sub>6</sub><sup>ˉ</sup> and MDM. This shift indicates that the P─F bond is weakened and the bond length of P─F is increased, as demonstrated by MD simulations (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202420573-fig-0002">2h,i</a>). The similar conclusion is also drawn from the <sup>19</sup>F-NMR spectra at 60&nbsp;°C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202420573-fig-0002">2k</a>). The chemical shift moves in the opposite direction to that of NaPF<sub>6</sub> after adding MDM, suggesting the enhanced salt dissociation in the electrolyte.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202420573-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0032_R_d29173719e803" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In other words, more DGM molecules are located in the solvation sheath. All the experimental data confirm the changes in the solvation structure and support the theoretical calculations.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202420573-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/49baeca3-2bc0-4442-9cd9-127d86f37774/anie202420573-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/49baeca3-2bc0-4442-9cd9-127d86f37774/anie202420573-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c8039694-1bc2-4326-863c-99228181693e/anie202420573-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/49baeca3-2bc0-4442-9cd9-127d86f37774/anie202420573-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202420573-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202420573" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">MDM prefers to bind anions rather than Na<sup>+</sup>. a) Snapshot and b) radial distribution functions of the solvation structures of Na<sup>+</sup> in BE+MDM, c) the coordination numbers of Na<sup>+</sup>⋯F(PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>) and Na<sup>+</sup>⋯O(DGM) in BE and in BE+MDM, d) the binding energies of Na<sup>+</sup> with PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>, DGM, and MDM, e) the binding energies of MDM with Na<sup>+</sup>, DGM, and PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>, f) isosurface maps of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>⋯MDM, g) scatter map of RDG (reduced density gradient) versus sign(<i>λ</i><sub>2</sub>)<i>ρ</i> (second Hessian eigenvalue, <i>λ</i><sub>2</sub>; electron density, <i>ρ</i>), h) Mulliken charge distribution on DGM and MDM near PF<sub>6</sub><sup>ˉ</sup>, i) the bond lengths of P─F in BE or in BE+MDM, j) Raman spectra, and k) <sup>19</sup>F NMR spectra of NaPF<sub>6</sub>, BE, and BE+MDM at 60&nbsp;°C. The concentration of MDM in BE+MDM was controlled to 5 wt% thereafter.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The association between PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> and MDM must significantly change the energy levels and the subsequent decomposition of the anion-related species. Therefore, we calculate HOMO/LUMO energy levels of the main species in the electrolyte, including the anion-related species. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202420573-fig-0003">3a</a>, the solvation effect of the cation/anion changes the energy levels and the electrochemical stability in thermodynamics. Using the energy levels of DGM as a reference, cation solvation usually lowers the energy levels of both HOMO and LUMO, especially that of LUMO, whereas anion solvation increases the energy levels, especially that of the HOMO. Thus, the electrochemical stability window (ESW) of an electrolyte is determined by the LUMO of the solvation structures of cations and the HOMO of the solvation structures of anion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202420573-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0033_R_d29173719e882" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Energy Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The preferential binding between MDM and PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> decreases the HOMO energy level of the anion-related species, compared to that of DGM and PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>. Therefore, the decomposition of the electrolyte greatly decreases at high voltages (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>). On the other hand, MDM also reduces the LUMO energy level of the cation-related species, which then facilitates the electrolyte reduction and the SEI formation on μ-Sn. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202420573-fig-0003">3b</a> shows the dissociation energy of P─F bonds before and after the association with MDM. Compared to free PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>, that associated with MDM has a smaller dissociation energy, which promotes the defluorination reaction and the formation of NaF in the SEI. This result is directly confirmed by the X-ray photoelectron spectra (XPS) of the electrode cycled in different electrolytes. In the C 1s spectra, the peaks at 284.1, 284.8, 286.2, 288.7, and 289.9&nbsp;eV can be ascribed to Si─C, C─C/C─H, C─O, O─C═O, and C─F (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202420573-fig-0003">3c,f</a>),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202420573-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0034_R_d29173719e907" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Energy Mater.">34</a>, <a href="#anie202420573-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0035_R_d29173719e907" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Nano Lett.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which basically originate from the SEI on μ-Sn, conductive carbon particles, and polymer binders. It is noted that the contents of C and O on the electrode cycled in BE+MDM are lower than those in BE (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>). Since the electrodes cycled in BE and BE+MDM share the same formulation, including active materials, conductive carbon particles, and polymer binders, this difference must be due to the SEI generated in different electrolytes. Therefore, the result suggests the less organics in the SEI and the reduced electrolyte decomposition in the case of BE+MDM. The difference between the cases of BE and BE+MDM in SEI is more obvious in the F 1s spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202420573-fig-0003">3d,g</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202420573-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0036_R_d29173719e919" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Nano Energy">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although the peak intensity of NaF gradually increases with Ar-ion sputtering in both cases, the content of NaF in BE+MDM is much higher than that in BE (11.1% vs. 3.65%, Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>). Rich-NaF SEI benefits the charge transfer and the cycling stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202420573-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0037_R_d29173719e927" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Energy Mater.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Finally, the peak of Si 2p is observed only in BE+MDM (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202420573-fig-0003">3e,h</a>), despite its low intensity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202420573-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0038_R_d29173719e936" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Chem. Eng. J.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This result is due to either the decomposition or the adsorption of MDM. Given the ductility and strength of Si─O, its presence on the electrode surface improves the mechanical strength.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202420573-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0039_R_d29173719e941" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Adv. Energy Mater.">39</a>, <a href="#anie202420573-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0040_R_d29173719e941" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - J. Power Sources">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The good compatibility and chemical stability of Si─O possibly inhibit the SEI dissolution, further improving the interfacial stability as confirmed later.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202420573-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ce6e23b5-b8b5-42f9-9cde-beff7b73f712/anie202420573-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/ce6e23b5-b8b5-42f9-9cde-beff7b73f712/anie202420573-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/0a96787c-7e4f-4afc-a179-ff23d4a06d58/anie202420573-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ce6e23b5-b8b5-42f9-9cde-beff7b73f712/anie202420573-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202420573-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202420573" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">MDM facilitates the formation of inorganic-rich SEI, improving the structure's integrity. a) The energy levels of HOMO and LUMO of different species in BE+MDM, b) the dissociation energy of P─F in PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> and in PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>⋯MDM, c)–h) the XPS spectra of the electrodes made of μ-Sn and cycled in different electrolytes, c), f) C 1s, d), g) F 1s, and e), h) Si 2p, i) Young's modulus of the electrodes made of μ-Sn and cycled in different electrolytes, j) voltage profiles of these electrodes before and after stored in a fully discharged state at 60&nbsp;°C, k) the charge capacity of these electrodes before and after the storage at high temperatures.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The improved mechanical properties by the SEI in BE+MDM are demonstrated by the Young's modulus. The working electrode made of μ-Sn was taken out from the half cells after 3 cycles at 60&nbsp;°C. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202420573-fig-0003">3i</a>, the average Young's modulus of the electrode cycled in BE+MDM is as high as 9.0&nbsp;GPa, much higher than the data cycled in BE (∼2.6&nbsp;GPa). The high Young's modulus indicates the increased resistance to intrinsic stress.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202420573-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0013_R_d29173719e978" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nano Lett.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the electrode surface cycled in BE+MDM is relatively flat (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13b</a>). Nevertheless, that cycled in BE is rough (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13a</a>) due to inhomogeneous swelling, crack formation, and so on. The similar results are also observed in the SEM images. Before cycles, the electrode made of μ-Sn is dense, homogeneous, and firmly attached to the current collector (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14a</a>). After several cycles in BE, the electrode breaks and becomes detached from the current collector (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14b</a>). In contrast, the electrode cycled in BE+MDM is compact and closely attached to the current collector (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14c</a>).</p>
         
         <p>The reduced SEI dissolution in BE+MDM is supported by the control experiments. The half cell of μ-Sn||Na was cycled at 60&nbsp;°C for 10 cycles at a constant current of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> to generate SEI.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202420573-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0041_R_d29173719e1004" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - ACS Energy Lett.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Then, the cell was discharged to 0.01&nbsp;V and stored in an open circuit for 100&nbsp;h. The small voltage variation after this storage comes either from the deviation from the thermodynamic equilibrium or from the possible dissolution of SEI components. In our case, the voltage increase in BE+MDM is 90.8&nbsp;mV, slightly smaller than 97.0&nbsp;mV in BE (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202420573-fig-0003">3j</a>). This indicates that the kinetic deviation could be negligible in two cases. However, the capacity losses in the subsequent charging process are 42.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> in BE+MDM and 298 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> in BE (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202420573-fig-0003">3k</a>). This difference implies that the SEI formed in BE has an inferior stability. If some of the SEI components dissolve during the storage, the electrolyte decomposition has to continue and passivate the electrode again. This aggravates the electrolyte depletion and increases the irreversible capacity loss,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202420573-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0042_R_d29173719e1020" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which could be attributed to the rich organic matter and the absence of Si─O and Na─F in the SEI formed in BE. The gradual dissolution and repeated formation of SEI make it thick and rough, as visualized by HRTEM. HRTEM images show that there is an uneven SEI layer with its thickness in the range of 12.4–23.8&nbsp;nm on the particle in BE (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15a</a>). In stark contrast, the SEI layer on the particles cycled in BE+MDM is thin (∼5&nbsp;nm) and uniform in thickness (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15b</a>). The small thickness of the SEI and the high content of NaF make SEI excellent performance in charge transfer at the interface (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>) and mechanical properties (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202420573-fig-0003">3i</a>). As displayed in the EIS spectra, the half cells using BE+MDM as the electrolyte show the reduced interfacial impedance (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16b</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202420573-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0043_R_d29173719e1044" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the cells using BE+MDM show a smaller activation energy (<i>E</i><sub>a</sub>) in the charge-transfer process than those using BE (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>), which explains the rapid charge transfer at the interface.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202420573-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0044_R_d29173719e1056" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Adv. Energy Mater.">44</a>, <a href="#anie202420573-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0045_R_d29173719e1056" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>In addition to the effect on the SEI, MDM also affects the side reactions in the electrolyte, using the hydrolysis reaction of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> as a model. The variation in Gibbs free energy variation (Δ<i>G</i>) in the hydrolysis reactions of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> and MDM are calculated (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202420573-fig-0004">4a</a>). The Δ<i>G</i> of the hydrolysis reaction is negative for MDM but positive for PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>. This indicates that H<sub>2</sub>O would preferentially react with MDM rather than PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>. Meanwhile, MDM is also able to spontaneously remove the traces of HF in the electrolyte (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202420573-fig-0004">4b</a>), thereby reducing the electrolyte corrosion to the SEI and electrode materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202420573-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0017_R_d29173719e1089" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Energy Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A validation experiment is then conducted by adding a small amount of water in BE or in BE+MDM (∼1000&nbsp;ppm). The electrolytes were stored at 60&nbsp;°C for 10&nbsp;days. As seen in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202420573-fig-0004">4c</a>, the electrolytes are clear at the beginning. However, BE becomes milky white after 3&nbsp;days and black after 10 days, suggesting the severe hydrolysis reactions, whereas BE+MDM remains clear throughout the whole storage, indicating the superior stability to moisture. This conclusion is further supported by the pH data. The pH of BE is strongly acidic after 10&nbsp;days, while that of BE+MDM is still neutral. The <sup>19</sup>F NMR spectra and mass spectra of the electrolytes are measured to demonstrate the enhanced stability of BE+MDM. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202420573-fig-0004">4d</a>, the chemical shift at −167.84&nbsp;ppm is from HF, and those at −78.44 and −81.08&nbsp;ppm are from POF<sub>3</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202420573-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0044_R_d29173719e1105" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Adv. Energy Mater.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Both of them are considered to be the hydrolysis products of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>. The high intensity suggests the heavy hydrolysis in BE. While in BE+MDM, these peaks almost disappear in the spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202420573-fig-0004">4e</a>), indicating the inhibited hydrolysis by MDM. The elimination of HF by MDM is witnessed by trimethylfluorosilane (TMSF) at −154.7&nbsp;ppm.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202420573-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0019_R_d29173719e1116" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Energy Storage Mater.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The same conclusion is also verified by the mass spectra (MS). In BE, there are many new peaks in the spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202420573-fig-0004">4f</a>), except that of [Na(DGM)]<sup>+</sup> at <i>m</i>/<i>z</i>&nbsp;=&nbsp;157.2. These peaks, for example, the strong peak of POF<sub>3</sub> (<i>m</i>/<i>z</i>&nbsp;=&nbsp;104), imply severe side reactions in the electrolyte.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202420573-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0046_R_d29173719e1138" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Adv. Energy Mater.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> On the contrary, these peaks related to side reactions are almost absent in the case of BE+MDM (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202420573-fig-0004">4g</a>), confirming the good inhibition of MDM on the hydrolysis reaction.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202420573-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/96b925d5-cbfd-4ed0-a0f7-1d14f824d339/anie202420573-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/96b925d5-cbfd-4ed0-a0f7-1d14f824d339/anie202420573-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c5d6b673-402d-4776-b4cf-949891f35d41/anie202420573-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/96b925d5-cbfd-4ed0-a0f7-1d14f824d339/anie202420573-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202420573-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202420573" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">MDM effectively mitigates the electrolyte corrosion to the electrodes. a) The variation of Gibbs free energy (Δ<i>G</i>) in the hydrolysis reaction of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> and MDM, b) the variation of the Gibbs free energy in the elimination reaction of HF by MDM, c) the optical images and pH variation of BE and BE+MDM after the addition of water. d), e) <sup>19</sup>F NMR spectra and (f, g) mass spectra of BE and BE+MDM after storage at 60&nbsp;°C for 10 days, d), f) BE, e), g) BE+MDM.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Scheme&nbsp;<a href="#anie202420573-fig-0006">1</a> summarizes the pathways for MDM to improve the performance. MDM does not bind Na<sup>+</sup>, but interacts with anions. Therefore, it weakens the P─F bond of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>ˉ</sup>, promotes the P─F cleavage, and facilitates the formation of NaF-rich SEI. The association of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>ˉ</sup>⋅⋅⋅MDM also retards anion migration and indirectly increases the transference number of Na<sup>+</sup> in the electrolyte. The association also affects the energy levels of HOMO/LUMO, extending the ESW. The in-depth investigation reveals that this association basically originates from the enhanced hydrogen bonds caused by the electron donating of Si→CH<sub>3</sub>. On the other hand, MDM exhibits high activity to scavenge H<sub>2</sub>O/HF, thereby reducing the electrolyte corrosion. All these results cooperate to advance the high-temperature performance of SIBs.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202420573-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/39169c0b-e691-4e8f-996f-eb0e41c8ae16/anie202420573-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/39169c0b-e691-4e8f-996f-eb0e41c8ae16/anie202420573-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/46d89fb9-acbe-47f2-9771-432d09a866c1/anie202420573-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/39169c0b-e691-4e8f-996f-eb0e41c8ae16/anie202420573-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Scheme 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202420573-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202420573" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Brief illustration of how MDM improves the high-temperature performance.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The electrochemical performance using MDM as the electrolyte additive was fully explored as follows. First of all, the optimized amount of MDM in the electrolyte needs to be identified. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, the electrolyte viscosity increases, but the ionic conductivity decreases with the increasing of MDM. The association of MDM with anions retards their migration, indirectly improving the transference number of Na<sup>+</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202420573-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202420573-bib-0047_R_d29173719e1225" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Adv. Mater.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Then, we measured the cycling performance of the half cells with different amounts of MDM in the electrolyte. It is found that 5 wt% of MDM (BE+MDM), which is the saturated concentration in the electrolyte, shows the longest cycle life (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a>). Therefore, the subsequent discussion is carried out based on this concentration. Firstly, the cycling performance of the half cells using BE+MDM is examined at room temperature. μ-Sn||Na exhibits a stable cycling up to 1600 cycles at 2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202420573-fig-0005">5a</a>), much better than the case of BE. The reversible capacity stepwise increases with the increasing temperature (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>). This result could be attributed to enhanced charge transfer and reaction kinetics at high temperatures. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202420573-fig-0005">5b</a> shows the voltage profiles of μ-Sn in the first cycle at 60&nbsp;°C. The charge capacity is approximately 830&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> in BE+MDM, very close to the theoretical specific capacity (847 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) of Sn. Then, the cycling stability of the half cells using BE+MDM is examined at 60&nbsp;°C. The discharge capacity of μ-Sn decays significantly in BE after 120 cycles at 2 A g<sup>−1</sup>, accompanied by a large variation of coulombic efficiency (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202420573-fig-0005">5c</a>). In contrast, the case using BE+MDM as the electrolyte keeps a reversible capacity of 752 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 750 cycles. Encouraged by the excellent electrochemical performance, the high loading of μ-Sn is examined to demonstrate its application potential. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202420573-fig-0005">5d</a>, the thick electrode with a mass loading of 4.9&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> delivers an area capacity of 4.3 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> at a current density of 0.98&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>. Furthermore, there is little capacity degradation upon cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a>). In order to exclude the influence of sodium metal in half cells, the symmetric cells of Sn||Sn were assembled to test the cycling performance at 60&nbsp;°C. As displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a>, the cell using BE+MDM as the electrolyte represents a stable cycling. While that using BE shows a rapid increase in electrode polarization due to the repeated growth of SEI on μ-Sn. This contrast confirms that the improved performance does not come from Na metal but from Sn.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202420573-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ae096ec4-6795-42a3-99a3-71f69fb010e6/anie202420573-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/ae096ec4-6795-42a3-99a3-71f69fb010e6/anie202420573-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c2ff2221-c5d2-4e57-a20e-15703974f66f/anie202420573-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ae096ec4-6795-42a3-99a3-71f69fb010e6/anie202420573-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202420573-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202420573" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of μ-Sn in BE+MDM at high temperatures. a) Cycling performance of μ-Sn in BE or in BE+MDM at room temperature. b) Voltage profiles of μ-Sn in the first cycle at 60&nbsp;°C using BE or BE+MDM as the electrolyte. c) Cycling performance of μ-Sn in BE or in BE+MDM at 60&nbsp;°C. d) Cycling performance of μ-Sn at a high mass loading. e)–g) Cycling performance of e) NFPP@C||Sn, f) NFPP@C||Bi, and g) NFPP@C||HC in BE or in BE+MDM at 60&nbsp;°C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Finally, the electrochemical performance of the full cells was assessed using Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)@C (NFPP@C) as the cathode material (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a>). As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202420573-fig-0005">5e</a>, the full cell cycled in BE+MDM displays a good stability with a capacity retention of 80.8% after 250 cycles at 60&nbsp;°C using a current density of 2 A g<sup>−1</sup>. While the capacity retention in the case of BE is only 27.2%. The similar advantage induced by MDM is also identified at room temperature. The full cell using BE+MDM retains a reversible capacity of 394&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 500 cycles at 3 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25</a>). In contrast, the full cell using BE delivers a specific capacity of 189 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> under the same conditions. Using MDM as the electrolyte additive also significantly enhances the rate capability of the full cells at different temperatures (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26</a>). BE+MDM is also applied for the high-temperature performance of other anode materials, μ-Bi and HC. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27a</a>, the iCE of μ-Bi||Na at 60&nbsp;°C is only 58.7% in BE and ∼71.0% in BE+MDM. The cycling ends after 1300 cycles in BE (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27b</a>) but sustains over 2000 cycles in BE+MDM. The full cell using BE+MDM delivers a reversible capacity of 274&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 300 cycles at 3 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202420573-fig-0005">5f</a>), resulting in a capacity retention of 85.3%. While the cycle lifespan using BE is only 40 cycles. BE+MDM as the electrolyte also improves the high-temperature performance of HC in SIBs. At 60&nbsp;°C, the capacity of HC rapidly decays in BE after 500 cycles and remains 47.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 2500 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S28</a>). The case in BE+MDM exhibits a capacity of 243 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> under the same conditions. The full cell of NFPP@C||HC in BE+MDM also presents better cycling stability than those in BE (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202420573-fig-0005">5g</a>). The results show the good compatibility of MDM with different anode materials.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202420573-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202420573-sec-0030-title"> Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In conclusion, a rational protocol is proposed to screen the potential electrolyte additives from various siloxane molecules for high-temperature SIBs. Then, MDM as the result of this screening is further investigated in detail. Theoretical calculations indicate that MDM does not enter into the solvation sheath of Na<sup>+</sup>, but prefers to interact with PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> due to the distinct electron donating of Si→CH<sub>3</sub> in the siloxane molecules. As a result, MDM weakens the P─F bond of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> and promotes the formation of NaF-rich SEI. Thus, the mechanical properties and charge-transfer kinetics related to SEI are improved significantly. In addition, MDM shows the high chemical activity with H<sub>2</sub>O so that the hydrolysis reaction of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> is suppressed at high temperature. All these benefits together boost the high-temperature performance of μ-Sn. Using commercial Sn microparticles as a model, μ-Sn delivers a specific capacity of 752 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 750 cycles in BE+MDM at 60&nbsp;°C, which is much better than the case without MDM. This high-temperature performance also surpasses the reported data of μ-Sn. This improved performance is also confirmed in full cells at high temperature. MDM as the electrolyte additive could also be adopted to other anode materials, such as Bi and HC. These results confirm the successful screening of siloxane molecules.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
